    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b6135116a2a94bb382727d8db4cf1c8c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3f1be5dc-89f9-4831-ab8d-52fce35c0a8e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:longzhen75@aliyun.com"><span>longzhen75@aliyun.com</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xji@csu.edu.cn"><span>xji@csu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Long/Zhen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ji/Xiaobo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a11" data-db-target-for="a11" aria-controls="a11" aria-haspopup="true" id="a11_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaobo Ji<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a11" aria-labelledby="a11_Ctrl" role="region" id="a11">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Xiaobo Ji</p>

                     </ul>
                     <p>College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Central South University, Changsha, 410083 China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:longzhen75@aliyun.com"><span>longzhen75@aliyun.com</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xji@csu.edu.cn"><span>xji@csu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ji/Xiaobo">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d183167668" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Ni/Mn-based oxide cathode materials have drawn great attention due to their high discharge voltage and large capacity, but structural instability at high potential causes rapid capacity decay. How to moderate the capacity loss while maintaining the advantages of high discharge voltage remains challenging. Herein, the replacement of Mn ions by Ga ions is proposed in the P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode for improving their cycling performances without sacrificing the high discharge voltage. With the introduction of Ga ions, the relative movement between the transition metal ions is restricted and more Na ions are retained in the lattice at high voltage, leading to an enhanced redox activity of Ni ions, validated by ex situ synchrotron X-ray absorption spectrum and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy. Additionally, the P2-O2 phase transition is replaced by a P2-OP4 phase transition with a smaller volume change, reducing the lattice strain in the <i>c</i>-axis direction, as detected by operando/ex situ X-ray diffraction. Consequently, the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.74</sub>Ga<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibits a high discharge voltage close to that of the undoped materials, while increasing voltage retention from 79% to 93% after 50 cycles. This work offers a new avenue for designing high-energy density Ni/Mn-based oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307225-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307225-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Due to their high specific capacity and environmental friendliness, layer-structured sodium transition metal oxides have attracted interest as candidates for sodium-ion battery cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0001_R_d183167660e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0005_R_d183167660e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> One of the advantages of layered materials is the variety of structures, for example, P2, O3, P3, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0006_R_d183167660e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem.">6</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0008_R_d183167660e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, this also makes the layered materials susceptible to phase transitions during electrochemical de-/sodiation of Na ions, resulting in structural instability as well as poor diffusion kinetics. Fortunately, for layered sodium transition metal oxides, there are many available transition metal (TM) ions with different redox potentials, which permits arbitrary combinations of TM ions and controls phase structures based on cationic potential to achieve the desired properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0009_R_d183167660e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Sci. Bull.">9</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0011_R_d183167660e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the various available TM ions, the combination of Ni with Mn is more attractive with its high discharge voltage, which implies a potentially high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0012_R_d183167660e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0014_R_d183167660e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, their practical application is seriously hampered by the poor cyclic stability when involving the high voltage range, which is derived from the structural instability caused by the multi-phase transformation. For example, in P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the P2-O2 phase transition occurs at a voltage platform of 4.1&nbsp;V, accompanied by large volume changes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0015_R_d183167660e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">15</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0017_R_d183167660e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other problem is that, apart from Ni ions, anions are also involved in redox reactions at high-voltage platforms, which causes cation migration as well as interfacial instability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0018_R_d183167660e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0019_R_d183167660e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The combination of these two factors can trigger intragranular cracking, structural damage, and surface-side reactions, leading to rapid capacity loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0020_R_d183167660e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0021_R_d183167660e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nanomaterials">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Currently, much effort is devoted to addressing the structural stability of the bulk phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0022_R_d183167660e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Energy Chem.">22</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0023_R_d183167660e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both elemental substitution and metal oxide coating have been proven to be effective strategies. For example, Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, and Zn<sup>2+</sup> are utilized to substitute Ni ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0024_R_d183167660e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Mater.">24</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0027_R_d183167660e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the introduction of these ions, the P2-O2 phase transition can be inhibited in Ni/Mn-based materials. In parallel, the OP4 (intermediate phase) phase is generally formed upon deep desodiation. This phase transformation with small volume changes mitigates the structural damage to some extent. In addition, there is a substitution of Mn ions or Ni/Mn ions with higher valence ions such as Ti<sup>4+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>, which has a similar function to the former ion substitution, showing a suppressive effect on the O2 phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0028_R_d183167660e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Commun.">28</a>-<a href="#smll202307225-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0030_R_d183167660e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, if these ions (Li<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>) are substituted in small amounts, the suppression of phase transition is less satisfactory. If the substitution is large, the high voltage capacity is greatly reduced, lowering the output voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0031_R_d183167660e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0032_R_d183167660e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Also, for Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, and Fe<sup>3+</sup>-substituted cathode materials, the lattice volume change remains large in OP4 phase transition process. Secondly, anionic redox reactions also seem to be unresolved in these kinds of ion-doped materials, which is detrimental to long-cycle performances.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0033_R_d183167660e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A">33</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0034_R_d183167660e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, O2 phase transition can be effectively prevented by Al<sup>3+</sup> doping even at low doping levels, which may stem from the stronger electronegativity of Al ions, giving it a stronger bond to the oxygen ion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0035_R_d183167660e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small Methods">35</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0036_R_d183167660e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, homogeneous Al doping is difficult and Al accumulation is easily formed on the surface, which is unfavorable to the cell performance. Although Al cannot be doped uniformly, this gives a good inspiration to search for similar ions. Ga element has a similar external electron structure to Al. The difference is that it has a stronger electronegativity and a larger ionic radius than the Al ion, suggesting a stronger Ga-O bond energy, which is expected to provide better structural regulation. Furthermore, a key reason for previous approaches to work is the substitution of Ni ions with other ions, which essentially reduces the Ni content by an average of around 10%, thereby suppressing the phase transition. Given this, a more straightforward strategy is to design the cathode with 20% Ni content and replace the Mn ions with other ions.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.74</sub>Ga<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials with uniform Ga ions distribution are prepared with the calcination method. The effect of Ga ions doping on the sodium storage mechanism is explored by ex situ X-ray absorption spectrum and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy. The redox activity of Ni ions at high voltage is increased and the structural stability of the transition metal layer can be enhanced by Ga ion doping. The structure evaluation analysis obtained by operando/ex situ X-ray diffraction reveals that the O2 phase transition and the accompanying irreversible structural changes are effectively suppressed at high-voltage platforms. Consequently, compared to undoped materials, the capacity retention increases from 46% to 76% and the median voltage retention increases from 79% to 93% after 50 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307225-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307225-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307225-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202307225-sec-0030-title">2.1 Phase Structure and Element Valence</h3>
            
            <p>For convenience, the undoped and Ga-doped samples prepared by the high-temperature solid-phase method are designated as NM and NMG. ICP-OES results show that the compositions of NM and NMG are Na<sub>0.60</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.79</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.60</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.74</sub>Ga<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD confirms that both materials are P2-layered structures without impurities (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0001">1a</a>). XRD Rietveld refinement was carried out by utilizing the FullProf method. The results show that in the Ga-doped sample, the transition metal layer shrinks while the <i>a</i>-axis and cell volume are expanded (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a>, Supporting Information). These effects can be attributed to the strong electronegativity of the Ga ion and its large ionic radius (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, diffraction peaks of sodium gallium oxide are detected by XRD when the Ga content reaches 10% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The morphology, microstructure, and elemental distribution of both materials are further explored by SEM, TEM, and EDS. Both NM and NMG are composed of disk-shaped particles with a diameter of ≈5&nbsp;µm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the select area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern and typical high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) image of NMG. The diffraction spots indicate the P2-type structure of the single NMG particles. The clear lattice fringe spacings of 2.49 Å agree well with the (100) planes of the P2 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0037_R_d183167660e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, EDS characterization of both materials demonstrates that the Ga ion is successfully introduced into the bulk of NMG materials, and all elements are well distributed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0001">1c</a>, and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/47263a7d-b663-424c-8a46-0efbef05d3d7/smll202307225-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/47263a7d-b663-424c-8a46-0efbef05d3d7/smll202307225-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bbc05a27-d46a-4e50-956f-372f66d2cd50/smll202307225-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/47263a7d-b663-424c-8a46-0efbef05d3d7/smll202307225-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rietveld refinement of XRD patterns for a) NM, and b) NMG materials, c) The SAED pattern and Typical HRTEM image of NMG materials, and d) The corresponding EDS images of NMG materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to determine the valence and content of the elements in both materials, XPS, XAS, and electrochemical analyses were performed. XPS patterns of NMG exhibit a clearly visible Ga <i>2p</i> peak, in good line with the EDS results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, The Ni <i>L</i>-edge spectrum pattern of both samples almost overlapped with that of NiO, showing that the Ni valence state is +2 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). By fitting the Mn <i>L</i>-edge spectra of NM and NMG samples, it is found that the average valence of Mn is +3.73 and +3.87, respectively (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). The elevated Mn valence of the Ga-doped sample resulted from the replacement of Mn by the lower valence Ga. Based on the proportion of metal ions in the ICP results and the valence state measured by XAS, the Na content of NM and NMG materials can be deduced to be 0.6 and 0.58 respectively. Although the Na percentage for the NM is approximately the same as the ICP results, the Na percentage for the NMG is lower than the ICP test values. To precisely evaluate the initial Na concentration, the as-prepared electrode is discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V first and then charged to 4.5&nbsp;V at 10&nbsp;µA in a coin-type cell. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0002">2c,d</a> shows that the discharge capacities of the two materials are 94 and 85&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>-1</sup>, corresponding to 0.362 and 0.327 Na. Considering that the Na layer cannot be completely removed and embedded in both materials, the Na content was determined to be 0.60 and 0.59, which is in good accordance with the XAS results. The relatively high percentage of Na in the ICP results for NMG may be derived from the surface, suggesting some residual alkali left on the surface of NMG.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e687c659-83ca-4cb7-a883-0bd9fd1c8bc4/smll202307225-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e687c659-83ca-4cb7-a883-0bd9fd1c8bc4/smll202307225-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a57e4ae4-9640-4688-8f31-1b728c1c384e/smll202307225-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e687c659-83ca-4cb7-a883-0bd9fd1c8bc4/smll202307225-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Element valence and sodium content analysis; fitted Mn L-edge XAS spectra of a) NM and b) NMG materials, The discharge–charge curves of c) NM and d) NMG materials at a current density of 10&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>-1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307225-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202307225-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performances</h3>
            
            <p>Electrochemical tests show a great discrepancy in the Na storage properties of the two materials, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0003">3</a>. NM and NMG materials have average discharge voltages of 3.54 and 3.53&nbsp;V and deliver capacities of 136 and 101&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>-1</sup>, respectively. However, the NM electrode suffers from rapid capacity loss and the NMG electrode remains almost constant for the first 10 cycles. Significantly, the reversible capacities of the Ga-doped materials at high-voltage plateaus are maintained, as well as the median voltage retention increases from 79% to 93%. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0003">3c</a>). The capacity retention is increased from 46% to 76% after 50 cycles compared to o-NM, demonstrating the beneficial effect of Ga ion doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0003">3i</a>). The CV curves show that NM and NMG materials undergo similar redox processes during the charge/discharge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Both materials have two pairs of redox peaks at 3.7 and 4.1&nbsp;V. To further verify the role of Ga ions, a cut-off voltage of 4.0&nbsp;V is set to exclude the influence of high-voltage plateaus. Interestingly, both NM and NMG materials have excellent cycling stability, suggesting good structural stability in the range of 2.5–4.0&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the factors causing the specific capacity loss of both materials within 2.5–4.2&nbsp;V are derived from the material's failure at high voltages. Obviously, one of the roles of Ga ions is the enhancement of structural reversibility of Ni/Mn-based materials at high-voltage plateaus. On the other hand, the high-rate capacities of NM materials within 2.5–4.0&nbsp;V are higher than that of NMG materials, implying the negative effect of Ga ions on Na ion diffusion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0003">3d,e</a> shows the charge/discharge curves of the two materials with different current densities at 2.5–4.2&nbsp;V. Apparently, there is a more rapid capacity decay at the high-voltage plateau with the increased current density, indicating that both materials suffer from sluggish Na ion diffusion kinetics in this region. This is also evidenced by the results of the GITT test, that is, the ion diffusion coefficient <i>D</i> values in the platform region are two orders of magnitude lower compared to the slope region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0003">3g,h</a>). It is worth noting that there is a remarkable discrepancy in the <i>D</i>-values of NM and NMG materials at the discharge plateau. The <i>D</i> value at the discharge plateau of NMG materials is an order of magnitude higher than that of NM materials. Thus, Ga ions have a strong influence on the ion transport path during charging/discharging in the high-voltage region.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a72a10dd-c763-44c8-a0d3-d3834cd5771c/smll202307225-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a72a10dd-c763-44c8-a0d3-d3834cd5771c/smll202307225-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2dab546b-a923-421f-ab21-619521ca7d38/smll202307225-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a72a10dd-c763-44c8-a0d3-d3834cd5771c/smll202307225-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The charge–discharge curves of a) NM and b) NMG. c) The median voltage of two materials as a function of cycles. d–f) The rate capacities of two materials from 0.1—5 C, the GITT results, and corresponding diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i>) values of g) NM and h) NMG materials. i) Cycling performances at 0.1 C (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>-1</sup>).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307225-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202307225-sec-0050-title">2.3 Redox Mechanism Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The valence states of Ni and Mn ions were examined by ex situ hard XAS as well as XPS during cycling. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0004">4a,d</a> shows the normalized Ni Kedge spectra of the two materials at different charge states. As charging and discharging proceed, the absorption edges of both materials are first shifted to higher energy positions and then return to their original positions, confirming the oxidation of Ni<sup>2+</sup> to the higher valence state. The difference is that the energy shifts of the Ni K-edge spectra of NM and NMG are 0.6 and 1.2&nbsp;eV, respectively. In addition, for NM materials, large bulges are observed in the pre-edge of Ni K-edge, implying a dramatic structural change in the local coordination of the Ni ions at the end of charging. The shift of the ex situ Ni 2p XPS spectra provides further evidence of the involvement of Ni ions for charge compensation in the NMG electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the redox process of Mn ions is also observed by ex situ XPS spectra results. From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0004">4c,f</a>, The positions of Mn 2<i>p</i> spectra for both materials remain unchanged in the region above 4.0&nbsp;V, demonstrating that the Mn ions maintain +4 above 4.0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0038_R_d183167660e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Am. Mineral.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mn 2<i>p</i> spectra are shifted to the low energy positions when discharging to 2.5&nbsp;V, suggesting the Mn<sup>+3/+4</sup> redox in the 2.5&nbsp;–4.0&nbsp;V region.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ee195c7a-1549-47bf-973c-d74c26e6623d/smll202307225-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ee195c7a-1549-47bf-973c-d74c26e6623d/smll202307225-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/482a9c38-fe52-447f-a215-5252aaf36dfa/smll202307225-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ee195c7a-1549-47bf-973c-d74c26e6623d/smll202307225-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synchrotron Ni K-edge XANES spectra of a) NM and d) NMG at different voltages, the corresponding magnitude of the FT Ni K-edge EXAFS spectra of b) NM and e) NMG, and c,f) Ex situ Mn 2<i>p</i> XPS spectra obtain from NM and NMG.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307225-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202307225-sec-0060-title">2.4 Evolution of Local Structure within Transition Metal Layer</h3>
            
            <p>Ni K-edge EXAFS spectra during the Na ion extraction/insertion process are also acquired to resolve the evolution of the local structure, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0004">4d,e</a>. In layered sodium transition metal oxides, the peak positions and magnitudes in the R-space are linearly dependent on the spacing and number of the coordination ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0039_R_d183167660e916" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Rev. Mod. Phys.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the electrode potential is increased to 3.9&nbsp;V, there is negligible variation in the peak position and magnitude in the <i>R</i>-space for NM materials, suggesting the stable local structure of Ni ions, which is well consistent with the outstanding cycle performances at 2.4–4.0&nbsp;V. When charged to 4.2&nbsp;V, the peak intensities are significantly reduced while the peak positions are shifted rarely, indicating that the coordinated ions are migrated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0040_R_d183167660e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the electrode potential returns to 3.9&nbsp;V, the profiles fail to overlap exactly that of the charged state. The intensity of the peak representing the Ni-O coordination is reduced, illustrating the irreversibility of the local structure. Compared to NM, the EXAFS intensity of NMG materials at the end of charging is almost constant, confirming that the migration of coordination ions in the transition metal layer is effectively restricted. Additionally, the position of the peak corresponding to Ni-O is shifted to the left when charged to 4.2&nbsp;V, implying a reduction in the average spacing between Ni and O ions. Since the spacing between the Ni ion and the TM ion remains unchanged, the shift in peak position can be considered as the migration of the oxygen ion, which is derived from the anion redox. It is well known that anion redox usually occurs at high voltages in Ni/Mn-based materials, leading to the migration of oxygen ions to form oxygen dimers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0041_R_d183167660e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Storage Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the changes in the local structure of both NM and NMG materials at the end of the charge should be associated not only with Ni ions redox but also with the anion redox. After experiencing a high voltage plateau, the local structure of NM materials is substantially damaged, resulting in irreversible structural changes, whereas the local structure of NMG materials is well maintained during charging.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307225-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202307225-sec-0070-title">2.5 Evolution of Average Structure</h3>
            
            <p>The operando XRD technique was carried out to elucidate the phase evolution of the NMG, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0005">5</a>. The gradual shift of the individual diffraction peaks as the Na ions are removed suggests a typical insertion/exclusion reaction mechanism. Noticeably, a new diffraction peak located at 17.2<sup>o</sup> emerges when charging to a high voltage plateau, which is an index to the (004) of the OP4 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0013_R_d183167660e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Power Sources">13</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0042_R_d183167660e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ. Sci.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further investigate the effect of Ga ion doping on the structural stability of the Ni/Mn-based materials, ex situ XRD of both NM and NMG materials was conducted, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0006">6</a>. For NM, the P2 phase is converted to the O2 phase when charged to 4.2&nbsp;V, similar to the previous Ni/Mn-based materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0043_R_d183167660e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, for Ga-doped materials, the P2-OP4 phase transition occurs when the voltage reaches 4.1 and above, which reduces the cell volume change. The changes in the cell parameters for the two materials before and after the phase transition are calculated (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). When charged to 4.2&nbsp;V, P2-type NM materials are converted into O2-type NM materials (<i>a</i> = 2.4734 Å, <i>c</i> = 9.01 Å) with a cell volume change of 22%. Meanwhile, OP4-type NMG materials (<i>a</i> = 2.4629 Å, <i>c</i> = 20.82 Å) show a small cell volume change of 8%. Another aspect that differs from the NM materials is that the OP4 phase disappears quickly during the discharge stage. The discharge stage of NMG materials consists of a voltage slope with a voltage platform. At the end of the voltage ramp at 4.07&nbsp;V, the structure of NMG is transformed to the P2 phase, suggesting an asymmetrical phase evolution path. Based on the XRD results, it is concluded that the high voltage plateau of NMG can be attributed to the P2-OP4 phase transition. Combined with previous reports about the Ni/Mn-based materials, it is found that the role of Ga ions acts in a similar way to Cu, Fe, Mg, Al, and Zn ions, that is, by forming the intermediate phase OP4 or Z phase to inhibit the formation of the O2 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307225-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0012_R_d183167660e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0044_R_d183167660e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem. Eng. J.">44</a>, <a href="#smll202307225-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307225-bib-0045_R_d183167660e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast to the other ion-doped materials, NMG still retains a high-voltage plateau, yielding high discharge voltages. Furthermore, the difference between these dopant elements on the capacity at high voltage is also compared (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Ga-doped materials show a large reversible capacity of about 39 mAh/g at high-voltage platforms.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e67faecb-fe2b-444b-a96e-a70e904d4385/smll202307225-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e67faecb-fe2b-444b-a96e-a70e904d4385/smll202307225-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/37d1013b-bcdf-48cc-87ed-4a02631f3510/smll202307225-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e67faecb-fe2b-444b-a96e-a70e904d4385/smll202307225-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Operando XRD results of NMG electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/34ea5750-7c76-4ebd-bb8d-76dcb7c100b0/smll202307225-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/34ea5750-7c76-4ebd-bb8d-76dcb7c100b0/smll202307225-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d7278901-74db-41f6-9138-891848e8f615/smll202307225-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/34ea5750-7c76-4ebd-bb8d-76dcb7c100b0/smll202307225-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XRD results for a) NM and b) NMG electrode at the first cycle.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307225-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307225-sec-0080-title">3 Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Based on the results above, the migration of ions within the transition metal layer and interlayer sliding can be effectively restricted by Ga ion doping, resulting in improved capacity retention. In order to gain a deeper understanding of the influences of Ga ions on structural evolution, the actual Na content of both NM and NMG materials at different potentials was quantified (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307225-fig-0007">7a</a>). For NM, a stoichiometric ratio of 0.256 Na ions is removed when charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, corresponding to the oxidation of all Mn ions to +4 and 36% of Ni ions to +3. After the P-O phase transition, the Na content drops to 0.045, equivalent to 0.299 moles of electron transfer, which can be attributed to the oxidation of Ni ions and anions. Since the XAS results show that the absorption edge of Ni shifted rarely and the local structure of Ni varies considerably, it is proposed that in the high-voltage region, anion redox is predominant and Ni redox is limited. Therefore, the irreversible structural changes of NM materials at high voltages can be ascribed to the coupling of anionic redox reactions with interlayer sliding, which is detrimental to the stability of the surface/interface. Comparatively, a 0.242 stoichiometric ratio of Na is removed for NMG at 4.0&nbsp;V, corresponding to the oxidation of all Mn ions to +4 and 59% of Ni ions to +3, which is slightly higher than the quantity of Ni redox in NM. In the high-voltage region, however, only 0.182 Na ions are taken off, which is smaller than the amount removed from NM. Since additional Na ions remain in the lattice, the prismatic coordination can be stabilized, which accounts for the inhibition of the P2-O2 transition and the formation of the OP4 phase. in addition, the dQ/dV curves at very low currents were conducted to explore the discrepancies in the kinetics of the phase transition. as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0007">7b</a>. An anomaly is the increased polarization of the voltage plateau in NMG materials. Electrochemical reactions at high-voltage platforms include electron transport, Na ion migration, and phase transitions. Since both NM and NMG experience O-type phase transitions by interlayer sliding, the kinetics of the interlayer sliding can be assumed to be consistent. From the GITT results, the Na ion diffusion coefficient of NMG is lower than that of NM at the middle of the high-voltage plateau. Therefore, the voltage hysteresis of NMG can be attributed to the slow Na ion migration between the P2 and OP4 phases. Finally, EIS analysis of both materials was performed to investigate the interface stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307225-fig-0007">7c,d</a>). Both materials are activated by three cycles at low current and then looped by 20 cycles at large current. The electrochemical charge transfer impedance of NM materials is increased rapidly and is much higher than that of the NMG material, regardless of whether it is cycled at low or high currents (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The increase in the interfacial charge transfer impedance of NM materials reflects the continuous generation of interfacial layers, which arises from the instability of the interfacial structure, resulting in a rapid loss of capacity. In contrast, the slow impedance growth of NMG demonstrates enhanced structural stability, consistent with its good cycling performances.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307225-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0bcd2a5b-22ac-4cff-9ab4-d4dd265e600e/smll202307225-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0bcd2a5b-22ac-4cff-9ab4-d4dd265e600e/smll202307225-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3785346c-b3c7-4863-b6e8-5bd70cf5a6c2/smll202307225-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0bcd2a5b-22ac-4cff-9ab4-d4dd265e600e/smll202307225-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307225-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307225" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The sodium content of NM and NMG electrodes at different potentials, b) dQ/dV plots of two samples, EIS, and fitting curves of c) NM and d) NMG collected at the discharge end.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In general, Ga doping is employed to improve the Na storage properties of Ni/Mn-based materials by enhancing structural stability and reducing the generation of interfacial passivation layers. Specifically speaking, Ga ion doping allows more Na ions to stay in the lattice at high voltage, which facilitates NMG to maintain its P-type structure and inhibit the O2 phase transition. Secondly, despite the existence of OP4 phase transition, the transition metal layer remains structurally stable and the redox of Ni ions is supported, which enables a better reversibility of the high voltage platform of the Ga doping materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307225-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307225-sec-0090-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, Ga-doped P2-type Ni/Mn-based materials are successfully prepared to mitigate the capacity fading. The Ga doping materials show enhanced bulk phase and interfacial structural stability, leading to increased capacity retention. P2-O2 phase transition at a high-voltage platform is inhibited by allowing more Na ions to stay in the lattice. Simultaneously, the relative movement between the TM ions is restricted, which improves the structural stability of the transition metal layers. In addition, Ga doping also contributes to the enhancement of the reversibility of the redox of Ni ions at high voltages compared to undoped materials. Consequently, compared to undoped materials, the median voltage retention of NMG materials increases from 79% to 93% after 50 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307225-sec-0100-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
